## Transcript

- [00:00] Comedian Mike Birbiglia was having trouble with sleep.
- [00:03] Though not with the actual sleeping part -- one night, while staying in a hotel, he dreamed
- [00:07] that a guided missile was on its way to his bed, and in his dream, he jumped out the window
- [00:11] to escape it.
- [00:12] Unfortunately, he also did this not in his dream.
- [00:15] From the second floor.
- [00:16] And the window was not open.
- [00:17] This little episode cost him 33 stitches and a trip to a sleep specialist.
- [00:22] Mike now sleeps in zipped-up mummy bags for his own safety.
- [00:26] The lesson here?
- [00:27] Sleep is not some break time when your brain, or your body, just goes dormant.
- [00:31] Far from it.
- [00:32] In truth, sleep is just another state of consciousness.
- [00:35] And only in the past few decades have we begun to really plumb its depths -- from why we
- [00:39] sleep in the first place, to what goes on in our brains when we do, to what happens
- [00:43] when we cant sleep.
- [00:44] And there is a lot that science has to say about your dreams!
- [00:48] Talk about weird!
- [00:49] Its like Sigmund Freud meets Neil Gaiman.
- [00:51] So, even though it may seem like youre dead to the world, when you sleep, your perceptual
- [00:54] window remains slightly open.
- [00:57] And kinda like Mike Birbiglias hotel room window, a trip through it can make for a pretty
- [01:00] wild ride.
- [01:01] But for your safety and enjoyment, Im here to guide you through this state of consciousness,
- [01:05] where youll learn more than a few things about human mind, including your own.
- [01:08] And heres hoping you wont need any stitches when were through.
- [01:17] [INTRO]
- [01:21] Technically speaking, sleep is a periodic, natural, reversible and near total loss of
- [01:25] consciousness, meaning its different than hibernation, being in a coma, or in say, an
- [01:29] anesthetic oblivion.
- [01:30] Although we spend about a third of our lives sleeping, and we know that its essential
- [01:34] to our health and survival, there still isnt a scientific consensus for why we do it.
- [01:39] Part of it probably has to do with simple recuperation, allowing our neurons and other
- [01:42] cells to rest and repair themselves.
- [01:44] Sleep also supports growth, because thats when our pituitary glands release growth hormones,
- [01:47] which is why babies sleep all the time.
- [01:49] Plus, sleep has all kinds of benefits for mental function, like improving memory, giving
- [01:53] our brains time to process the events of the day, and boosting our creativity.
- [01:57] But even if were not quite sure of all the reasons why we sleep, technology has given
- [02:00] us great insight into how we sleep.
- [02:03] And for that we can thank little Armond Aserinsky.
- [02:06] One night in early 1950s Chicago, eight-year-old Armond was tucked into his bed by his father.
- [02:11] But this night, instead of getting a kiss on the forehead, little Armond got some electrodes
- [02:15] taped to his face.
- [02:16] Armonds dad was Eugene Aserinsky, a grad student looking to test out a new electroencephalograph,
- [02:21] or EEG machine, that measures the brains electrical activity.
- [02:25] That night, as his son slept peacefully, he watched the machine go bonkers with brain
- [02:30] wave patterns, and -- after making sure that his machine wasnt somehow broken -- discovered
- [02:34] that the brain doesnt just "power down" during sleep, as most scientists thought.
- [02:39] Instead, he had discovered the sleep stage we now call REM or rapid eye movement, a perplexing
- [02:43] period when the sleeping brain is buzzing with activity, even though the body is in
- [02:47] a deep slumber.
- [02:48] Aserinsky and his colleague Nathaniel Kleitman went on to become pioneers of sleep research.
- [02:53] Since then, sleep specialists armed with similar technology have shown that we experience four
- [02:58] distinct stages of sleep, each defined by unique brainwave patterns.
- [03:02] Say youre just going to bed.
- [03:03] All day your endocrine system has been releasing awake hormones like cortisol.
- [03:07] But with nightfall comes the release of sleepy melatonin hormones from the pineal gland.
- [03:12] Your brain is relaxed, but still awake, a level of activity that EEGs measure as alpha
- [03:16] waves.
- [03:17] Youre feeling sleepy, your breath slows, and suddenly youre asleep.
- [03:21] This exact moment is clearly evident on an EEG reading, as those alpha waves immediately
- [03:26] transition to the irregular non-Rapid Eye Movement stage one (NREM-1) waves.
- [03:31] Its in this first stage of sleep you might experience hypnagogic sensations -- those
- [03:36] brief moments when you feel like youre falling, and your body jerks, startling you.
- [03:40] As you relax more deeply, you move into NREM-2 stage sleep, as your brain starts exhibiting
- [03:45] bursts of rapid brain wave activity called sleep spindles.
- [03:48] Youre now definitely asleep, but you could still be easily awakened.
- [03:52] NREM-3 comes with slow rolling delta waves.
- [03:56] We now know that you can have brief and fragmentary dreams in the first three stages of sleep,
- [04:00] but eventually youll get to the most important stage: full REM sleep, that famous stage of
- [04:05] sugarplum slumber that makes eyeballs go nuts, grants vivid visual dreams, and provided the
- [04:10] namesake for a certain famous rock band.
- [04:12] REM sleep is kinda paradoxical.
- [04:14] Your motor cortex is jumping all over the place, but your brainstem is blocking those
- [04:18] messages, leaving your muscles so relaxed that youre basically paralyzed.
- [04:22] Except for your eyes.
- [04:23] That whole sleep cycle repeats itself every 90 minutes or so, transitioning back and forth
- [04:28] between the stages of sleep.
- [04:29] Obviously sleep is super important, and lack of sleep is terrible for your health, mental
- [04:34] ability, and mood.
- [04:35] In fact its a predictor for depression, and has been linked to things like weight
- [04:38] gain, as your hunger-arousing and -suppressing hormones get out of whack.
- [04:42] Sleep deprivation also causes immune system suppression, and slowed reaction time which
- [04:46] is why you should not drive sleepy.
- [04:48] Of course, a bad nights sleep here and there is part of life, but there are a host
- [04:51] of bona fide sleep disorders out there that can really make life pretty terrible, or in
- [04:55] Mike Birbiglias case, land you in the emergency room.
- [04:57] Weve got insomnia, which is persistent problems of falling or staying asleep.
- [05:01] And kind of its opposite, narcolepsy, whose sufferers sometimes experience brief, uncontrollable
- [05:05] attacks of overwhelming sleepiness, called sleep attacks.
- [05:08] This, as you can imagine, can get in the way of all sorts of things that you might enjoy
- [05:12] doing, like driving, eating, pole-vaulting.
- [05:15] Narcolepsy may have several different causes, including a deficiency in the neurotransmitter
- [05:19] hypocretin, which helps keep you awake.
- [05:21] But in more rare cases, brain trauma, infection, and disease may contribute to it as well.
- [05:25] So, thats rare, but you probably know someone with sleep apnea, the disorder that causes
- [05:29] sleepers to temporarily stop breathing, until their decreased oxygen levels wake them up.
- [05:34] Birbiglia, meanwhile, turned out to have a REM sleep behavior disorder, which we dont
- [05:37] fully understand yet, but appears to be associated with a dopamine deficiency.
- [05:41] Then weve got night terrors, which are as terrible as they sound...
- [05:45] spurring increased heart and breathing rates, screaming, and thrashing thats seldom remembered
- [05:49] upon waking.
- [05:51] Night terrors are most common in children under seven, and may be spurred by stress,
- [05:55] fatigue, sleep deprivation, and sleeping in unfamiliar surroundings.
- [05:58] Much like sleepwalking and sleeptalking, night terrors occur during the NREM-3 stage of sleep,
- [06:04] and are NOT the same as nightmares, which occur, like most dreaming, during REM sleep.
- [06:08] But oh, in REM sleep, what dreams may come...
- [06:12] There you are, running naked as your teeth fall out, being chased down the beach by a
- [06:16] Matt Damon centaur.
- [06:17] You wake up, feel around your mouth thinking what?
- [06:20] What?
- [06:21] What?!
- [06:22] WHAT?!
- [06:23] Welcome to your dreams, those vivid, emotional images racing through your sleeping brain,
- [06:27] often providing a backdrop so bizarre that it may seem like David Lynch, Terry Gilliam,
- [06:31] and Tim Burton are trying to out-weird each other in a film festival.
- [06:34] A really, really long festival, considering the average person spends about six years
- [06:39] of their lives dreaming.
- [06:40] So yeah, sometimes you have really crazy dreams.
- [06:43] But mostly, your average dream usually just sort of unpacks and reshuffles what you did
- [06:46] that day.
- [06:47] For example last night I dreamt about Tumblr, cause I spent a lot of time on Tumblr yesterday.
- [06:51] If you played Tetris all afternoon, you might dream of blocks falling from the sky.
- [06:55] If something traumatic happened to you, your brain might provide you with a nightmare to
- [06:58] help extinguish your daytime fears - Thanks, brain!
- [07:01] Then again...you might be unable to stop dreaming about the trauma, which well look at in
- [07:05] the future when we discuss post-traumatic stress disorder.
- [07:07] Our two-track minds of course allow us to register more stimuli than we outwardly acknowledge
- [07:11] during the day, and in that way, the sounds of car alarms or stinky dog farts that you
- [07:15] might not even have noticed may get incorporated into your dream, too.
- [07:18] And thats all interesting and weird and sometimes a little gross, but whats the
- [07:22] real purpose of dreaming?
- [07:24] Whyyy do we do this?
- [07:26] Well, as you might have guessed, theres more than one idea out there
- [07:29] The study of dreams is is a mix of neuroscience and psychology known as oneirology.
- [07:33] Oneiros is the Greek for dream, and if youre a Neil Gaiman fan you may recognize it as
- [07:38] one of the Sandmans many names.
- [07:39] The one that comes with a toga and Orpheuss head.
- [07:42] But Sandman aside, if you want to talk dreams, we might want to start with our old friend
- [07:45] Freud.
- [07:46] In his landmark 1900 book The Interpretation of Dreams, Freud proposed that our dreams
- [07:50] offer us wish-fulfillment.
- [07:52] He thought a dreams manifest content, the stuff you remember in the morning, was a sort
- [07:55] of censored and symbolic version of whatever inner conflict was really going on in that
- [08:00] dreams unconscious, or latent, content.
- [08:02] Not surprisingly, the wish-fulfillment theory lacks scientific chops and has for the most
- [08:06] part fallen out of favor -- because, really, you can interpret a dream any way you want.
- [08:10] Like, sometimes a cigar is just a cigar.
- [08:13] Luckily we have some other theories to consider.
- [08:15] The information processing theory proposes that our dreams help us sort out and process
- [08:19] the days events and fix them into our memories.
- [08:22] This may be particularly important when it comes to learning and remembering new information,
- [08:26] and some studies show that people recall new tasks better after a good REM sleep full of
- [08:31] dreams.
- [08:32] But if brainwave readings show us anything, its that theres a lot going on in your
- [08:35] brain when you dream, and the physiological function theory suggests that dreaming may
- [08:39] promote neural development and preserve neural pathways by providing the brain with stimulation.
- [08:44] When our brains are stimulated, they expand their connections more.
- [08:47] So, babies, for example, spend much of their sleep time dreaming, perhaps in part to help
- [08:51] their brain circuitry develop more quickly.
- [08:53] This is similar to the idea that dreams are part of our cognitive development.
- [08:57] By this model, dreams draw on our knowledge and understanding of the world, mimicking
- [09:01] reality, and engaging those same brain networks that light up when we daydream.
- [09:05] And finally, there are theories that focus on the way REM sleep triggers neural activity,
- [09:09] and the idea that dreams are just sort of accidental side-effects, the brains attempt
- [09:14] to weave a story out of a bunch of random sights, emotions, and memories -- which is
- [09:18] how in dreamland you might actually marry that Matt Damon centaur and give birth to
- [09:22] a baby with banana fingers and a raccoon tail.
- [09:24] For now scientists continue to debate the function of dreams, but one thing we know
- [09:28] for sure is that REM sleep is vital, both biologically and psychologically.
- [09:33] But, hey, you think your dreams are nut-bar?
- [09:35] Next week, were looking at other altered states of consciousness, where youll learn
- [09:39] what your brain really looks like on drugs, and whether you can actually hypnotize someone
- [09:43] to do your evil bidding or just act like a chicken.
- [09:46] For now, if youve stayed awake during this episode, you learned about the four stages
- [09:50] of sleep -- NREM 1, 2, 3 and REM itself -- as well as some major theories for the psychological
- [09:56] purpose of dreaming, including information processing, physiological function, cognitive
- [10:01] development, and neural activity models.
